<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Weather radar</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg/220px-Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg/330px-Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg/440px-Darwin_Ap_WF3_Radar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

Weather (WF44) radar dish</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OUPRIME1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="527" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/OUPRIME1.png/220px-OUPRIME1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/OUPRIME1.png/330px-OUPRIME1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/OUPRIME1.png/440px-OUPRIME1.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OUPRIME1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/University_of_Oklahoma" title="University of Oklahoma">University of Oklahoma</a> <a href="/wiki/OU-PRIME" title="OU-PRIME">OU-PRIME</a> C-band, polarimetric, weather radar during construction</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Weather radar</b>, also called <b>weather surveillance radar</b> (<b>WSR</b>) and <b>Doppler weather radar</b>, is a type of <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> used to locate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, calculate its motion, and estimate its type (rain, snow, <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a> etc.). Modern weather radars are mostly <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">pulse-Doppler radars</a>, capable of detecting the motion of rain droplets in addition to the intensity of the precipitation. Both types of data can be analyzed to determine the structure of storms and their potential to cause <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a>.</p>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Radar_in_World_War_II" title="Radar in World War II">World War II, radar</a> operators discovered that weather was causing echoes on their screen, masking potential enemy targets. Techniques were developed to filter them, but scientists began to study the phenomenon. Soon after the war, <a href="/wiki/Military_surplus" title="Military surplus">surplus</a> radars were used to detect precipitation. Since then, weather radar has evolved on its own and is now used by national weather services, research departments in universities, and in television newscasts. Raw images are routinely used and specialized software can take radar data to make short term <a href="/wiki/Weather_forecasting#Nowcasting" title="Weather forecasting">forecasts</a> of future positions and intensities of rain, snow, hail, and other weather phenomena. Radar output is even incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Numerical_weather_prediction" title="Numerical weather prediction">numerical weather prediction</a> models to improve analyses and forecasts.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="400" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg/170px-David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg/255px-David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg/340px-David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_Atlas_Weather_radar_pionneer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/David_Atlas" title="David Atlas">David Atlas</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During World War II, military radar operators noticed noise in returned echoes due to rain, snow, and <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">sleet</a>. After the war, military scientists returned to civilian life or continued in the Armed Forces and pursued their work in developing a use for those echoes. In the United States, <a href="/wiki/David_Atlas" title="David Atlas">David Atlas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> at first working for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">Air Force</a> and later for <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>, developed the first operational weather radars. In Canada, J.S. Marshall and R.H. Douglas formed the "Stormy Weather Group"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> in Montreal. Marshall and his doctoral student Walter Palmer are well known for their work on the drop size distribution in mid-latitude rain that led to understanding of the Z-R relation, which correlates a given radar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reflectivity" title="Reflectivity">reflectivity</a> with the rate at which rainwater is falling. In the United Kingdom, research continued to study the radar echo patterns and weather elements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratiform" title="Stratiform">stratiform</a> rain and <a href="/wiki/Cloud#Convective_lift" title="Cloud">convective clouds</a>, and experiments were done to evaluate the potential of different wavelengths from 1 to 10 centimeters. By 1950 the UK company <a href="/wiki/EKCO" title="EKCO">EKCO</a> was demonstrating its airborne 'cloud and collision warning search radar equipment'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1965May06_1919.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/1965May06_1919.jpg/220px-1965May06_1919.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/1965May06_1919.jpg/330px-1965May06_1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/1965May06_1919.jpg/440px-1965May06_1919.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1965May06_1919.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1960s radar technology detected tornado producing <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercells</a> over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis-Saint_Paul" title="Minneapolis-Saint Paul">Minneapolis-Saint Paul</a> metropolitan area.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1953 Donald Staggs, an electrical engineer working for the Illinois State Water Survey, made the first recorded radar observation of a "<a href="/wiki/Hook_echo" title="Hook echo">hook echo</a>" associated with a tornadic thunderstorm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1950 and 1980, reflectivity radars, which measure position and intensity of precipitation, were incorporated by weather services around the world. The early meteorologists had to watch a <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tube</a>. During the 1970s, radars began to be standardized and organized into networks. The first devices to capture radar images were developed. The number of scanned angles was increased to get a three-dimensional view of the precipitation, so that horizontal cross-sections (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAPPI" title="CAPPI">CAPPI</a>) and vertical cross-sections could be performed. Studies of the organization of thunderstorms were then possible for the <a href="/wiki/Alberta_Hail_Project" title="Alberta Hail Project">Alberta Hail Project</a> in Canada and <a href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Laboratory" title="National Severe Storms Laboratory">National Severe Storms Laboratory</a> (NSSL) in the US in particular.</p>
<p>The NSSL, created in 1964, began experimentation on dual <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> signals and on <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a> uses. In May 1973, a tornado devastated <a href="/wiki/Union_City,_Oklahoma" title="Union City, Oklahoma">Union City, Oklahoma</a>, just west of <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City" title="Oklahoma City">Oklahoma City</a>. For the first time, a Dopplerized 10 cm wavelength radar from NSSL documented the entire life cycle of the tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSL_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSL-5">[5]</a></sup> The researchers discovered a <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_meteorology" title="Mesoscale meteorology">mesoscale</a> rotation in the cloud aloft before the tornado touched the ground – the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tornadic_vortex_signature" title="Tornadic vortex signature">tornadic vortex signature</a>. NSSL's research helped convince the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> that Doppler radar was a crucial forecasting tool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSL_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSSL-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1974_Super_Outbreak" title="1974 Super Outbreak">Super Outbreak</a> of tornadoes on 3–4 April 1974 and their devastating destruction might have helped to get funding for further developments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Between 1980 and 2000, weather radar networks became the norm in North America, Europe, Japan and other developed countries. Conventional radars were replaced by Doppler radars, which in addition to position and intensity could track the relative velocity of the particles in the air. In the United States, the construction of a network consisting of 10 cm radars, called <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> or WSR-88D (Weather Surveillance Radar 1988 Doppler), was started in 1988 following NSSL's research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSL_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-NSSL-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In Canada, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_Canada" title="Environment Canada">Environment Canada</a> constructed the <a href="/wiki/King_City_weather_radar_station" title="King City weather radar station">King City</a> station,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> with a 5 cm research Doppler radar, by 1985; McGill University dopplerized its radar (<a href="/wiki/J._S._Marshall_Radar_Observatory" title="J. S. Marshall Radar Observatory">J. S. Marshall Radar Observatory</a>) in 1993. This led to a complete <a href="/wiki/Canadian_weather_radar_network" title="Canadian weather radar network">Canadian Doppler network</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-location_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-location-8">[8]</a></sup> between 1998 and 2004. France and other European countries had switched to Doppler networks by the early 2000s. Meanwhile, rapid advances in computer technology led to algorithms to detect signs of severe weather, and many applications for media outlets and researchers.</p>
<p>After 2000, research on dual polarization technology moved into operational use, increasing the amount of information available on precipitation type (e.g. rain vs. snow). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOLBO161_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-GOLBO161-9">[9]</a></sup> "Dual polarization" means that microwave radiation which is <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarized</a> both horizontally and vertically (with respect to the ground) is emitted. Wide-scale deployment was done by the end of the decade or the beginning of the next in some countries such as the United States, France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and Canada. In April 2013, all <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> NEXRADs were completely dual-polarized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dualpol-NOAA_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dualpol-NOAA-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2003, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> has been experimenting with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phased-array_radar" title="Phased-array radar">phased-array radar</a> as a replacement for conventional parabolic antenna to provide more time resolution in <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_sounding" title="Atmospheric sounding">atmospheric sounding</a>. This could be significant with severe thunderstorms, as their evolution can be better evaluated with more timely data.</p>
<p>Also in 2003, the National Science Foundation established the <a href="/wiki/Engineering_Research_Center_for_Collaborative_Adaptive_Sensing_of_the_Atmosphere" title="Engineering Research Center for Collaborative Adaptive Sensing of the Atmosphere">Engineering Research Center for Collaborative Adaptive Sensing of the Atmosphere</a> (CASA), a multidisciplinary, multi-university collaboration of engineers, computer scientists, meteorologists, and sociologists to conduct fundamental research, develop enabling technology, and deploy prototype engineering systems designed to augment existing radar systems by sampling the generally undersampled lower troposphere with inexpensive, fast scanning, dual polarization, mechanically scanned and phased array radars.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="How_a_weather_radar_works">How a weather radar works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: How a weather radar works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sending_radar_pulses">Sending radar pulses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sending radar pulses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-beam.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Radar-beam.svg/280px-Radar-beam.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Radar-beam.svg/420px-Radar-beam.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Radar-beam.svg/560px-Radar-beam.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-beam.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A radar beam spreads out as it moves away from the radar station, covering an increasingly large volume.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Weather radars send directional pulses of <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> radiation, on the order of a <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">microsecond</a> long, using a <a href="/wiki/Cavity_magnetron" title="Cavity magnetron">cavity magnetron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Klystron" title="Klystron">klystron</a> tube connected by a <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguide</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_antenna" title="Parabolic antenna">parabolic antenna</a>. The wavelengths of 1 – 10 cm are approximately ten times the diameter of the droplets or ice particles of interest, because <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> occurs at these frequencies. This means that part of the energy of each pulse will bounce off these small particles, back in the direction of the radar station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shorter wavelengths are useful for smaller particles, but the signal is more quickly attenuated. Thus 10 cm (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-band" title="S-band">S-band</a>) radar is preferred but is more expensive than a 5 cm <a href="/wiki/C_band_(IEEE)" title="C band (IEEE)">C-band</a> system. 3 cm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-band" title="X-band">X-band</a> radar is used only for short-range units, and 1 cm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ka-band" title="Ka-band">Ka-band</a> weather radar is used only for research on small-particle phenomena such as drizzle and fog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=W-band&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="W-band (page does not exist)">W-band</a> weather radar systems have seen limited university use, but due to quicker attenuation, most data are not operational.</p>
<p>Radar pulses spread out as they move away from the radar station. Thus the volume of air that a radar pulse is traversing is larger for areas farther away from the station, and smaller for nearby areas, decreasing <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">resolution</a> at far distances. At the end of a 150 – 200 km sounding range, the volume of air scanned by a single pulse might be on the order of a cubic kilometer. This is called the <i>pulse volume</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-vol_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-vol-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The volume of air that a given pulse takes up at any point in time may be approximated by the formula <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>h</mi>
<msup>
<mi>r</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<msup>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \,{v=hr^{2}\theta ^{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\, {v = h r^2 \theta^2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/31f3272bdf28c46d96034a2e72c9be0bf0f0a4ee" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:10.267ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>, where v is the volume enclosed by the pulse, h is pulse width (in e.g. meters, calculated from the duration in seconds of the pulse times the speed of light), r is the distance from the radar that the pulse has already traveled (in e.g. meters), and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \,\theta }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\,\theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5be774700ff4bae2ea1d75b8046b59b28a174b93" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.488ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the beam width (in radians). This formula assumes the beam is symmetrically circular, "r" is much greater than "h" so "r" taken at the beginning or at the end of the pulse is almost the same, and the shape of the volume is a cone <a href="/wiki/Frustum" title="Frustum">frustum</a> of depth "h".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Listening_for_return_signals">Listening for return signals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Listening for return signals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Between each pulse, the radar station serves as a receiver as it listens for return signals from particles in the air. The duration of the "listen" cycle is on the order of a <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">millisecond</a>, which is a thousand times longer than the pulse duration. The length of this phase is determined by the need for the microwave radiation (which travels at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>) to propagate from the detector to the weather target and back again, a distance which could be several hundred kilometers. The horizontal distance from station to target is calculated simply from the amount of time that elapses from the initiation of the pulse to the detection of the return signal. The time is converted into distance by multiplying by the speed of light in air:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>Distance</mtext>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\text{Distance}}=c{\frac {\Delta t}{2n}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\text{Distance} = c \frac{\Delta t}{2n}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c8ced860dd2bb917922e354e7cb4d4238a66dd4e" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:17.263ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>c</i> = 299,792.458 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Km/s" title="Km/s">km/s</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, and <i>n</i> ≈ 1.0003 is the refractive index of air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podesta_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Podesta-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>If pulses are emitted too frequently, the returns from one pulse will be confused with the returns from previous pulses, resulting in incorrect distance calculations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Determining_height">Determining height</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Determining height">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-hauteur-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="563" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Radar-hauteur-en.svg/330px-Radar-hauteur-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Radar-hauteur-en.svg/495px-Radar-hauteur-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Radar-hauteur-en.svg/660px-Radar-hauteur-en.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-hauteur-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The radar beam path with height</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Assuming the Earth is round, the radar beam in vacuum would rise according to the reverse curvature of the Earth. However, the atmosphere has a <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> that diminishes with height, due to its diminishing density. This bends the radar beam slightly toward the ground and with a <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Atmosphere" title="International Standard Atmosphere">standard atmosphere</a> this is equivalent to considering that the curvature of the beam is 4/3 the actual curvature of the Earth. Depending on the elevation angle of the antenna and other considerations, the following formula may be used to calculate the target's height above ground:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>H</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<msup>
<mi>r</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</mrow>

<mn>2</mn>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H={\sqrt {r^{2}+(k_{e}a_{e})^{2}+2rk_{e}a_{e}\sin(\theta _{e})}}-k_{e}a_{e}+h_{a},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="H = \sqrt{r^2+(k_ea_e)^2+2rk_{e}a_{e}\sin(\theta_e)} - k_{e}a_{e} + h_{a}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4cad4b9a8b8ed3cd6460173c605112e4f08c1941" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:49.913ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>r</i> = distance radar–target,</dd>
<dd><i>k</i><sub>e</sub> = 4/3,</dd>
<dd><i>a</i><sub>e</sub> = <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> radius,</dd>
<dd><i>θ</i><sub>e</sub> = <a href="/wiki/Elevation_(ballistics)" title="Elevation (ballistics)">elevation angle</a> above the <a href="/wiki/Radar_horizon" title="Radar horizon">radar horizon</a>,</dd>
<dd><i>h</i><sub>a</sub> = height of the feedhorn above ground.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="502" data-file-width="958" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png/220px-Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png/330px-Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png/440px-Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar_coverage_from_ground_level.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scanned volume by using multiple elevation angles</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A weather radar network uses a series of typical angles that will be set according to the needs. After each scanning rotation, the antenna elevation is changed for the next sounding. This scenario will be repeated on many angles to scan all the volume of air around the radar within the maximum range. Usually, this scanning strategy is completed within 5 to 10 minutes to have data within 15 km above ground and 250 km distance of the radar. For instance in Canada, the 5 cm weather radars use angles ranging from 0.3 to 25 degrees. The image to the right shows the volume scanned when multiple angles are used.</p>
<p>Due to the Earth's curvature and change of index of refraction with height, the radar cannot "see" below the height above ground of the minimal angle (shown in green) or closer to the radar than the maximal one (shown as a red cone in the center).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airbusradar_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-airbusradar-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calibrating_intensity_of_return">Calibrating intensity of return</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Calibrating intensity of return">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</math></span><img alt="P_{r}=P_{t}{{G^{2}\lambda ^{2}} \over {{(4\pi )}^{3}R^{4}}}{\frac  {c\tau }{2}}{\frac  {\pi R^{2}\theta ^{2}}{4}}\eta =P_{t}\tau G^{2}\lambda ^{2}\theta ^{2}{\frac  {c}{512(\pi ^{2})}}{\frac  {\eta }{R^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/52e71dd712d7a45198af4cd208c21a030278817d" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:54.841ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Which leads to:</p>

<p>Notice that the return now varies inversely to <b><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

In order to compare the data coming from different distances from the radar, one has to normalize them with this ratio.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_types">Data types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Data types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Reflectivity_(in_decibel_or_dBZ)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reflectivity_.28in_decibel_or_dBZ.29">Reflectivity (in decibel or dBZ)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reflectivity (in decibel or dBZ)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Return echoes from targets ("reflectivity") are analyzed for their intensities to establish the precipitation rate in the scanned volume. The wavelengths used (1–10 cm) ensure that this return is proportional to the rate because they are within the validity of <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> which states that the targets must be much smaller than the wavelength of the scanning wave (by a factor of 10).</p>
<p>Reflectivity perceived by the radar (Z<sub>e</sub>) varies by the sixth power of the rain droplets' diameter (D), the square of the dielectric constant (K) of the targets and the drop size distribution (e.g. N[D] of <i>Marshall-Palmer</i>) of the drops. This gives a truncated <a href="/wiki/Gamma_function" title="Gamma function">Gamma function</a>, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yau_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yau-19">[19]</a></sup> of the form:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mrow>

<p>Precipitation rate (R), on the other hand, is equal to the number of particles, their volume and their fall speed (v[D]) as:</p>
<dl>

</dl>
<p>So Z<sub>e</sub> and R have similar functions that can be resolved giving a relation between the two of the form:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Z = aR<sup>b</sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Where a and b depend on the type of precipitation (snow, rain, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convective" title="Convective">convective</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratiform" title="Stratiform">stratiform</a>), which has different <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Λ<!-- Λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ac0a4a98a414e3480335f9ba652d12571ec6733" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.623ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>, K, N<sub>0</sub> and v.</p>
<ul>
<li>As the antenna scans the atmosphere, on every angle of azimuth it obtains a certain strength of return from each type of target encountered. Reflectivity is then averaged for that target to have a better data set.</li>
<li>Since variation in diameter and dielectric constant of the targets can lead to large variability in power return to the radar, reflectivity is expressed in dBZ (10 times the logarithm of the ratio of the echo to a standard 1 mm diameter drop filling the same scanned volume).</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="How_to_read_reflectivity_on_a_radar_display">How to read reflectivity on a radar display</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: How to read reflectivity on a radar display">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Radar returns are usually described by colour or level. The colours in a radar image normally range from blue or green for weak returns, to red or magenta for very strong returns. The numbers in a verbal report increase with the severity of the returns. For example, the U.S. National Doppler Radar sites use the following scale for different levels of reflectivity:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>magenta: 65 dBZ (extremely heavy precipitation, possible hail)</li>
<li>red: 52 dBZ</li>
<li>yellow: 36 dBZ</li>
<li>green: 20 dBZ (light precipitation)</li>
</ul>
<p>Strong returns (red or magenta) may indicate not only heavy rain but also thunderstorms, hail, strong winds, or tornadoes, but they need to be interpreted carefully, for reasons described below.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Aviation_conventions">Aviation conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Aviation conventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>When describing weather radar returns, pilots, dispatchers, and air traffic controllers will typically refer to three return levels:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>level 1</b> corresponds to a green radar return, indicating usually light precipitation and little to no turbulence, leading to a possibility of reduced visibility.</li>
<li><b>level 2</b> corresponds to a yellow radar return, indicating moderate precipitation, leading to the possibility of very low visibility, moderate turbulence and an uncomfortable ride for aircraft passengers.</li>
<li><b>level 3</b> corresponds to a red radar return, indicating heavy precipitation, leading to the possibility of thunderstorms and severe turbulence and structural damage to the aircraft.</li>
</ul>
<p>Aircraft will try to avoid level 2 returns when possible, and will always avoid level 3 unless they are specially-designed research aircraft.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation_types">Precipitation types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Precipitation types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Some displays provided by commercial weather sites, like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Weather_Channel" title="The Weather Channel">The Weather Channel</a></i>, show precipitation types during the winter month : rain, snow, mixed precipitations (<a href="/wiki/Rain_and_snow_mixed" title="Rain and snow mixed">sleet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freezing_rain" title="Freezing rain">freezing rain</a>). This is not an analysis of the radar data itself but a post-treatment done with other data sources, the primary being surface reports (<a href="/wiki/METAR" title="METAR">METAR</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWC_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-TWC-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over the area covered by radar echoes, a program assigns a precipitation type according to the surface temperature and <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a> reported at the underlying <a href="/wiki/Weather_station" title="Weather station">weather stations</a>. Precipitation types reported by human operated stations and certain automatic ones (<a href="/wiki/Automated_airport_weather_station" title="Automated airport weather station">AWOS</a>) will have higher weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Then the program does interpolations to produce an image with defined zones. These will include <a href="/wiki/Interpolation" title="Interpolation">interpolation</a> errors due to the calculation. <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_meteorology" title="Mesoscale meteorology">Mesoscale</a> variations of the precipitation zones will also be lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWC_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-TWC-22">[22]</a></sup> More sophisticated programs use the <a href="/wiki/Numerical_weather_prediction" title="Numerical weather prediction">numerical weather prediction</a> output from models, such as <a href="/wiki/North_American_Mesoscale_Model" title="North American Mesoscale Model">NAM</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_Research_and_Forecasting_model" title="Weather Research and Forecasting model">WRF</a>, for the precipitation types and apply it as a first guess to the radar echoes, then use the surface data for final output.</p>
<p>Until dual-polarization (section <a href="#Polarization">Polarization</a> below) data are widely available, any precipitation types on radar images are only indirect information and must be taken with care.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Velocity">Velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-Doppler_winds.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="573" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Radar-Doppler_winds.png/220px-Radar-Doppler_winds.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Radar-Doppler_winds.png/330px-Radar-Doppler_winds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Radar-Doppler_winds.png/440px-Radar-Doppler_winds.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-Doppler_winds.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Idealized example of Doppler output. Approaching velocities are in blue and receding velocities are in red. Notice the sinusoidal variation of speed when going around the display at a particular range.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Pulse-Doppler radar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doppler_radar" title="Doppler radar">Doppler radar</a></div>
<p>Precipitation is found in and below clouds. Light precipitation such as drops and flakes is subject to the air currents, and scanning radar can pick up the horizontal component of this motion, thus giving the possibility to estimate the wind speed and direction where precipitation is present.</p>
<p>A target's motion relative to the radar station causes a change in the reflected frequency of the radar pulse, due to the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a>. With velocities of less than 70-metre/second for weather echos and radar wavelength of 10 cm, this amounts to a change only 0.1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>. This difference is too small to be noted by electronic instruments. However, as the targets move slightly between each pulse, the returned wave has a noticeable <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a> difference or <i>phase shift</i> from pulse to pulse.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pulse_pair">Pulse pair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pulse pair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Doppler weather radars use this phase difference (pulse pair difference) to calculate the precipitation's motion. The intensity of the successively returning pulse from the same scanned volume where targets have slightly moved is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>

</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>

<p>So <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This speed is called the radial Doppler velocity because it gives only the <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radial</a> variation of distance versus time between the radar and the target. The real speed and direction of motion has to be extracted by the process described below.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doppler_dilemma">Doppler dilemma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Doppler dilemma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="874" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png/260px-Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png/390px-Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png/520px-Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-Dilemne_Doppler.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Maximum range from reflectivity (red) and unambiguous Doppler velocity range (blue) with <a href="/wiki/Pulse_repetition_frequency" title="Pulse repetition frequency">pulse repetition frequency</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The phase between pulse pairs can vary from -<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\pi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9be4ba0bb8df3af72e90a0535fabcc17431e540a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.342ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> and +<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\pi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9be4ba0bb8df3af72e90a0535fabcc17431e540a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.342ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, so the unambiguous Doppler velocity range is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>V<sub>max</sub> = <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
