</math></span><img alt="\pm " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/869e366caf596564de4de06cb0ba124056d4064b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.819ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mrow>
<mn>4</mn>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\lambda }{4\Delta t}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\frac{\lambda}{4\Delta t}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/56d7fa20d5e54f769f7f8ef747d39e091998ed23" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:4.805ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This is called the <a href="/wiki/Nyquist_frequency" title="Nyquist frequency">Nyquist</a> velocity. This is inversely dependent on the time between successive pulses: the smaller the interval, the larger is the unambiguous velocity range. However, we know that the maximum range from reflectivity is directly proportional to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Delta t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Delta t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8c28867ecd34e2caed12cf38feadf6a81a7ee542" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.796ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>x = <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>c</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {c\Delta t}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\frac{c\Delta t}{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed26e04b890948b149d7d62904876c4fee3b4675" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:4.65ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The choice becomes increasing the range from reflectivity at the expense of velocity range, or increasing the latter at the expense of range from reflectivity. In general, the useful range compromise is 100–150 km for reflectivity. This means for a wavelength of 5 cm (as shown in the diagram), an unambiguous velocity range of 12.5 to 18.75 metre/second is produced (for 150 km and 100 km, respectively). For a 10 cm radar such as the NEXRAD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> the unambiguous velocity range would be doubled.</p>
<p>Some techniques using two alternating pulse repetition frequencies (PRF) allow a greater Doppler range. The velocities noted with the first pulse rate could be equal or different with the second. For instance, if the maximum velocity with a certain rate is 10 metre/second and the one with the other rate is 15 m/s. The data coming from both will be the same up to 10 m/s, and will differ thereafter. It is then possible to find a mathematical relation between the two returns and calculate the real velocity beyond the limitation of the two PRFs.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doppler_interpretation">Doppler interpretation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Doppler interpretation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="386" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png/220px-Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png/330px-Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-vents_radiaux_Doppler.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Radial component of real winds when scanning through 360 degrees</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a uniform rainstorm moving eastward, a radar beam pointing west will "see" the raindrops moving toward itself, while a beam pointing east will "see" the drops moving away. When the beam scans to the north or to the south, no relative motion is noted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Synoptic">Synoptic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Synoptic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale_meteorology" title="Synoptic scale meteorology">synoptic scale</a> interpretation, the user can extract the wind at different levels over the radar coverage region. As the beam is scanning 360 degrees around the radar, data will come from all those angles and be the radial projection of the actual wind on the individual angle. The intensity pattern formed by this scan can be represented by a cosine curve (maximum in the precipitation motion and zero in the perpendicular direction). One can then calculate the direction and the strength of the motion of particles as long as there is enough coverage on the radar screen.</p>
<p>However, the rain drops are falling. As the radar only sees the radial component and has a certain elevation from ground, the radial velocities are contaminated by some fraction of the falling speed. This component is negligible in small elevation angles, but must be taken into account for higher scanning angles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Meso_scale">Meso scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Meso scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the velocity data, there could be smaller zones in the radar coverage where the wind varies from the one mentioned above. For example, a thunderstorm is a <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_meteorology" title="Mesoscale meteorology">mesoscale</a> phenomenon which often includes rotations and <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a>. These may only cover few square kilometers but are visible by variations in the radial speed. Users can recognize velocity patterns in the wind associated with rotations, such as <a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclone</a>, convergence (<a href="/wiki/Outflow_boundary" title="Outflow boundary">outflow boundary</a>) and divergence (<a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downburst</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization">Polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-polarisation_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="713" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Radar-polarisation_en.svg/280px-Radar-polarisation_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Radar-polarisation_en.svg/420px-Radar-polarisation_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Radar-polarisation_en.svg/560px-Radar-polarisation_en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-polarisation_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Targeting with dual-<a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> will reveal the form of the droplet</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)#Hydrometeor" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Droplets of falling liquid water</a> tend to have a larger horizontal axis due to the <a href="/wiki/Drag_coefficient" title="Drag coefficient">drag coefficient</a> of air while falling (water droplets). This causes the water <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a> to be oriented in that direction; so, radar beams are, generally, polarized horizontally in order to receive the maximal signal reflection.</p>
<p>If two pulses are sent simultaneously with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthogonal" title="Orthogonal">orthogonal</a> polarization (vertical and horizontal, <i>Z<sub>V</sub></i> and <i>Z<sub>H</sub></i> respectively), two independent sets of data will be received. These signals can be compared in several useful ways:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAM_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAM-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Differential Reflectivity (<i>Z<sub>dr</sub></i>) – The differential reflectivity is the ratio of the reflected vertical and horizontal power returns as <i>Z<sub>V</sub></i>/<i>Z<sub>H</sub></i>. Among other things, it is a good indicator of drop shape and drop shape is a good estimate of average drop size.</li>
<li>Correlation Coefficient (<i>ρ<sub>hv</sub></i>) – A statistical correlation between the reflected horizontal and vertical power returns. High values, near one, indicate homogeneous precipitation types, while lower values indicate regions of mixed precipitation types, such as rain and snow, or hail, or in extreme cases debris aloft, usually coinciding with a <a href="/wiki/Tornado_vortex_signature" title="Tornado vortex signature">Tornado vortex signature</a>.</li>
<li>Linear Depolarization Ratio (<i>LDR</i>) – This is a ratio of a vertical power return from a horizontal pulse or a horizontal power return from a vertical pulse. It can also indicate regions where there is a mixture of precipitation types.</li>
<li>Differential Phase (<i>θ<sub>dp</sub></i>) – The differential phase is a comparison of the returned phase difference between the horizontal and vertical pulses. This change in phase is caused by the difference in the number of wave cycles (or wavelengths) along the propagation path for horizontal and vertically polarized waves. It should not be confused with the Doppler frequency shift, which is caused by the motion of the cloud and precipitation particles. Unlike the differential reflectivity, correlation coefficient and linear depolarization ratio, which are all dependent on reflected power, the differential phase is a "propagation effect." It is a very good estimator of rain rate and is not affected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attenuation_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Attenuation (electromagnetic radiation)">attenuation</a>. The range derivative of differential phase (specific differential phase, <i>K<sub>dp</sub></i>) can be used to localize areas of strong precipitation/attenuation.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>With more information about particle shape, dual-polarization radars can more easily distinguish airborne debris from precipitation, making it easier to locate <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornados</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>With this new knowledge added to the reflectivity, velocity, and spectrum width produced by Doppler weather radars, researchers have been working on developing algorithms to differentiate precipitation types, non-meteorological targets, and to produce better rainfall accumulation estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAM_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-TAM-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the U.S., <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Atmospheric_Research" title="National Center for Atmospheric Research">NCAR</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Laboratory" title="National Severe Storms Laboratory">NSSL</a> have been world leaders in this field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAM_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-TAM-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPOLE_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPOLE-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Oceanographic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanographic and Atmospheric Administration">NOAA</a> established a test deployment for dual-polametric radar at NSSL and equipped all its 10 cm <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> radars with dual-polarization, which was completed in April 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dualpol-NOAA_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dualpol-NOAA-11">[11]</a></sup> In 2004, <a href="/wiki/ARMOR_Doppler_Weather_Radar" title="ARMOR Doppler Weather Radar">ARMOR Doppler Weather Radar</a> in Huntsville, Alabama was equipped with a SIGMET Antenna Mounted Receiver, giving Dual-Polarmetric capabilities to the operator. <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a> <a href="/wiki/J._S._Marshall_Radar_Observatory" title="J. S. Marshall Radar Observatory">J. S. Marshall Radar Observatory</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montreal,_Canada" title="Montreal, Canada">Montreal, Canada</a> has converted its instrument (1999)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and the data are used operationally by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_Canada" title="Environment Canada">Environment Canada</a> in Montreal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Another Environment Canada radar, in <a href="/wiki/King_City,_Ontario" title="King City, Ontario">King City</a> (North of <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>), was dual-polarized in 2005;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIMMS_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIMMS-32">[32]</a></sup> it uses a 5 cm wavelength, which experiences greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attenuation_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Attenuation (electromagnetic radiation)">attenuation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Environment Canada is working on converting all of its radars to dual-polarization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EN-2012_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-EN-2012-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9t%C3%A9o-France" title="Météo-France">Météo-France</a> is planning on incorporating dual-polarizing Doppler radar in its network coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_types_of_radar_outputs">Main types of radar outputs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Main types of radar outputs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All data from radar scans are displayed according to the need of the users. Different outputs have been developed through time to reach this. Here is a list of common and specialized outputs available.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plan_position_indicator">Plan position indicator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Plan position indicator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plan_position_indicator" title="Plan position indicator">Plan position indicator</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1196" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/260px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/390px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/520px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thunderstorm line viewed in reflectivity (dBZ) on a PPI</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since data are obtained one angle at a time, the first way of displaying them has been the Plan Position Indicator (PPI) which is only the layout of radar return on a two dimensional image. One has to remember that the data coming from different distances to the radar are at different heights above ground.</p>
<p>This is very important as a high rain rate seen near the radar is relatively close to what reaches the ground but what is seen from 160 km away is about 1.5 km above ground and could be far different from the amount reaching the surface. It is thus difficult to compare weather echoes at different distances from the radar.</p>
<p>PPIs are afflicted with ground echoes near the radar as a supplemental problem. These can be misinterpreted as real echoes. So other products and further treatments of data have been developed to supplement such shortcomings.</p>
<p>Usage: Reflectivity, Doppler and polarimetric data can use PPI.</p>
<p>In the case of Doppler data, two points of view are possible: relative to the surface or the storm. When looking at the general motion of the rain to extract wind at different altitudes, it is better to use data relative to the radar. But when looking for rotation or wind shear under a thunderstorm, it is better to use the storm relative images that subtract the general motion of precipitation leaving the user to view the air motion as if he would be sitting on the cloud.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constant-altitude_plan_position_indicator">Constant-altitude plan position indicator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Constant-altitude plan position indicator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-angles.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="880" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Radar-angles.png/260px-Radar-angles.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Radar-angles.png/390px-Radar-angles.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Radar-angles.png/520px-Radar-angles.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-angles.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Typical angles scanned in Canada. The zigzags represent data angles used to make CAPPIs at 1.5 km and 4 km of altitude.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constant_altitude_plan_position_indicator" title="Constant altitude plan position indicator">Constant altitude plan position indicator</a></div>
<p>To avoid some of the problems on PPIs, the constant-altitude plan position indicator (CAPPI) has been developed by Canadian researchers. It is basically a horizontal cross-section through radar data. This way, one can compare precipitation on an equal footing at difference distance from the radar and avoid ground echoes. Although data are taken at a certain height above ground, a relation can be inferred between ground stations' reports and the radar data.</p>
<p>CAPPIs call for a large number of angles from near the horizontal to near the vertical of the radar to have a cut that is as close as possible at all distance to the height needed. Even then, after a certain distance, there isn't any angle available and the CAPPI becomes the PPI of the lowest angle. The zigzag line on the angles diagram above shows the data used to produce 1.5 km and 4 km height CAPPIs. Notice that the section after 120 km is using the same data.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Usage</dt>
</dl>
<p>Since the CAPPI uses the closest angle to the desired height at each point from the radar, the data can originate from slightly different altitudes, as seen on the image, in different points of the radar coverage. It is therefore crucial to have a large enough number of sounding angles to minimize this height change. Furthermore, the type of data must be changing relatively gradually with height to produce an image that is not noisy.</p>
<p>Reflectivity data being relatively smooth with height, CAPPIs are mostly used for displaying them. Velocity data, on the other hand, can change rapidly in direction with height and CAPPIs of them are not common. It seems that only <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a> is producing regularly Doppler CAPPIs with the 24 angles available on their radar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGill2_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGill2-36">[36]</a></sup> However, some researchers have published papers using velocity CAPPIs to study <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a> and development of <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harasti_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harasti-37">[37]</a></sup> Finally, polarimetric data are recent and often noisy. There doesn't seem to have regular use of CAPPI for them although the <i>SIGMET</i> company offer a software capable to produce those types of images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRIS_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRIS-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Real-time examples</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.radar.mcgill.ca/imagery/radar-animation.html" rel="nofollow">McGill University</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://weather.ec.gc.ca/radar/index_e.html?id=WMN" rel="nofollow">Environment Canada</a></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertical_composite">Vertical composite</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Vertical composite">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baserefl_composite_comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="1141" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Baserefl_composite_comparison.png/220px-Baserefl_composite_comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Baserefl_composite_comparison.png/330px-Baserefl_composite_comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Baserefl_composite_comparison.png/440px-Baserefl_composite_comparison.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baserefl_composite_comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Base PPI versus Composite.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Composite_reflectivity" title="Composite reflectivity">Composite reflectivity</a></div>
<p>Another solution to the PPI problems is to produce images of the maximum reflectivity in a layer above ground. This solution is usually taken when the number of angles available is small or variable. The American <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> is using such Composite as their scanning scheme can vary from 4 to 14 angles, according to their need, which would make very coarse CAPPIs. The Composite assures that no strong echo is missed in the layer and a treatment using Doppler velocities eliminates the ground echoes. Comparing base and composite products, one can locate <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updrafts</a> zones.</p>
<p>Real time example: <a class="external text" href="http://radar.weather.gov/ridge/radar.php?rid=cxx&amp;product=N0R&amp;overlay=11101111&amp;loop=no" rel="nofollow">NWS Burlington radar, one can compare the BASE and COMPOSITE products</a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accumulations">Accumulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Accumulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">Rain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-accumulations_eng.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="624" data-file-width="800" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-accumulations_eng.png/260px-Radar-accumulations_eng.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-accumulations_eng.png/390px-Radar-accumulations_eng.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-accumulations_eng.png/520px-Radar-accumulations_eng.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-accumulations_eng.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
24 hours rain accumulation on the Val d'Irène radar in Eastern Canada. Notice the zones without data in the East and Southwest caused by radar beam blocking from mountains.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another important use of radar data is the ability to assess the amount of precipitation that has fallen over large basins, to be used in <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrological</a> calculations; such data is useful in flood control, sewer management and dam construction. The computed data from radar weather may be used in conjunction with data from ground stations.</p>
<p>To produce radar accumulations, we have to estimate the rain rate over a point by the average value over that point between one PPI, or CAPPI, and the next; then multiply by the time between those images. If one wants for a longer period of time, one has to add up all the accumulations from images during that time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Echotops">Echotops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Echotops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aviation is a heavy user of radar data. One map particularly important in this field is the Echotops for flight planning and avoidance of dangerous weather. Most country weather radars are scanning enough angles to have a 3D set of data over the area of coverage. It is relatively easy to estimate the maximum altitude at which precipitation is found within the volume. However, those are not the tops of clouds as they always extend above the precipitation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertical_cross_sections">Vertical cross sections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Vertical cross sections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To know the vertical structure of clouds, in particular thunderstorms or the level of the melting layer, a vertical cross-section product of the radar data is available. This is done by displaying only the data along a line, from coordinates A to B, taken from the different angles scanned.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_Height_Indicator">Range Height Indicator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Range Height Indicator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When a weather radar is scanning in only one direction vertically, it obtains high resolution data along a vertical cut of the atmosphere. The output of this sounding is called a <i>Range Height Indicator</i> (RHI) which is excellent for viewing the detailed vertical structure of a storm. This is different from the vertical cross section mentioned above by the fact that the radar is making a vertical cut along specific directions and does not scan over the entire 360 degrees around the site. This kind of sounding and product is only available on research radars.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radar_networks">Radar networks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Radar networks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg/260px-Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg/390px-Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg/520px-Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berrimah-radar-darwin-nt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Berrimah Radar in <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin, Northern Territory</a> Australia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Over the past few decades, radar networks have been extended to allow the production of composite views covering large areas. For instance, many countries, including the United States, Canada and much of Europe, produce images that include all of their radars. This is not a trivial task.</p>
<p>In fact, such a network can consist of different types of radar with different characteristics such as beam width, wavelength and calibration. These differences have to be taken into account when matching data across the network, particularly to decide what data to use when two radars cover the same point. If one uses the stronger echo but it comes from the more distant radar, one uses returns that are from higher altitude coming from rain or snow that might evaporate before reaching the ground (<a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a>). If one uses data from the closer radar, it might be attenuated passing through a thunderstorm. Composite images of precipitations using a network of radars are made with all those limitations in mind.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Automatic_algorithms">Automatic algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Automatic algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-algorithme_eng.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="237" data-file-width="305" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-algorithme_eng.png/260px-Radar-algorithme_eng.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Radar-algorithme_eng.png 1.5x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-algorithme_eng.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The square in this Doppler image has been automatically placed by the radar program to spot the position of a <a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclone</a>. Notice the inbound/outbound doublet (blue/yellow) with the zero velocity line (gray) parallel to the radial to the radar (up right). It is noteworthy to mention that the change in wind direction here occurs over less than 10 km.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To help meteorologists spot dangerous weather, mathematical algorithms have been introduced in the weather radar treatment programs. These are particularly important in analyzing the Doppler velocity data as they are more complex. The polarization data will even need more algorithms.</p>
<p>Main algorithms for reflectivity:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-9"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vertically_integrated_liquid" title="Vertically integrated liquid">Vertically Integrated Liquid</a> (VIL) is an estimate of the total mass of precipitation in the clouds.</li>
<li><i>VIL Density</i> is VIL divided by the height of the cloud top. It is a clue to the possibility of large hail in thunderstorms.</li>
<li><i>Potential wind gust</i>, which can estimate the winds under a cloud (a downdraft) using the VIL and the height of the echotops (radar estimated top of the cloud) for a given storm cell.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">Hail</a> algorithms that estimate the presence of hail and its probable size.</li>
</ul>
<p>Main algorithms for Doppler velocities:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-10"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">Mesocyclone</a> detection: it is triggered by a velocity change over a small circular area. The algorithm is searching for a "<i>doublet</i>" of inbound/outbound velocities with the zero line of velocities, between the two, <u>along</u> a radial line from the radar. Usually the mesocyclone detection must be found on two or more stacked progressive tilts of the beam to be significative of rotation into a thunderstorm cloud.</li>
<li>TVS or <a href="/wiki/Tornado_vortex_signature" title="Tornado vortex signature">Tornado Vortex Signature</a> algorithm is essentially a mesocyclone with a large velocity threshold found through many scanning angles. This algorithm is used in <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> to indicate the possibility of a tornado formation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">Wind shear</a> in low levels. This algorithm detects variation of wind velocities from point to point in the data and looking for a <i>doublet</i> of inbound/outbound velocities with the zero line <u>perpendicular</u> to the radar beam. The wind shear is associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downdraft" title="Downdraft">downdraft</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downburst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microburst" title="Microburst">microburst</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gust_front" title="Gust front">gust fronts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a> under thunderstorms.</li>
<li>VAD Wind Profile (VWP) is a display that estimates the direction and speed of the horizontal wind at various upper levels of the atmosphere, using the technique explained in the Doppler section.</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animations">Animations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Animations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animated_hurricane.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1c/Animated_hurricane.gif" width="100"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animated_hurricane.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
PPI reflectivity loop (in dBZ) showing the evolution of a hurricane</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The animation of radar products can show the evolution of reflectivity and velocity patterns. The user can extract information on the dynamics of the meteorological phenomena, including the ability to extrapolate the motion and observe development or dissipation. This can also reveal non-meteorological artifacts (false echoes) that will be discussed later.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radar_Integrated_Display_with_Geospatial_Elements">Radar Integrated Display with Geospatial Elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Radar Integrated Display with Geospatial Elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="640" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif/220px-Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif/330px-Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif/440px-Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joplin-tornado-2308utc-radar.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the RIDGE presentation of <a href="/wiki/2011_Joplin_tornado" title="2011 Joplin tornado">2011 Joplin tornado</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A new popular presentation of weather radar data in United States is via <i>Radar Integrated Display with Geospatial Elements</i> (RIDGE) in which the radar data is projected on a map with geospatial elements such as topography maps, highways, state/county boundaries and weather warnings. The projection often is flexible giving the user a choice of various geographic elements. It is frequently used in conjunction with animations of radar data over a time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Limitations_and_artifacts">Limitations and artifacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Limitations and artifacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-artefacts.PNG"><img alt="Radar-artefacts.PNG" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="321" data-file-width="700" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Radar-artefacts.PNG/400px-Radar-artefacts.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Radar-artefacts.PNG/600px-Radar-artefacts.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Radar-artefacts.PNG 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-artefacts.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Radar data interpretation depends on many hypotheses about the atmosphere and the weather targets, including:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Atmosphere" title="International Standard Atmosphere">International Standard Atmosphere</a>.</li>
<li>Targets small enough to obey the Rayleigh scattering, resulting in the return being proportional to the precipitation rate.</li>
<li>The volume scanned by the beam is full of <i><b>meteorological</b></i> targets (rain, snow, etc.), all of the same variety and in a uniform concentration.</li>
<li>No <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_(optics)" title="Absorption (optics)">attenuation</a></li>
<li>No amplification</li>
<li>Return from side lobes of the beam are negligible.</li>
<li>The beam is close to a <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian function</a> curve with power decreasing to half at half the width.</li>
<li>The outgoing and returning waves are similarly polarized.</li>
<li>There is no return from multiple reflections.</li>
</ul>
<p>These assumptions are not always met; one must be able to differentiate between reliable and dubious echoes.</p>
<h3><span id="Anomalous_propagation_(non-standard_atmosphere)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Anomalous_propagation_.28non-standard_atmosphere.29">Anomalous propagation (non-standard atmosphere)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Anomalous propagation (non-standard atmosphere)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anomalous_propagation" title="Anomalous propagation">Anomalous propagation</a></div>
<p>The first assumption is that the radar beam is moving through air that cools down at a certain rate with height. The position of the echoes depend heavily on this hypothesis. However, the real atmosphere can vary greatly from the norm.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Super_refraction">Super refraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Super refraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_inversion" title="Temperature inversion">Temperature inversions</a> often form near the ground, for instance by air cooling at night while remaining warm aloft. As the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_of_refraction" title="Index of refraction">index of refraction</a> of air decreases faster than normal the radar beam bends toward the ground instead of continuing upward. Eventually, it will hit the ground and be reflected back toward the radar. The processing program will then wrongly place the return echoes at the height and distance it would have been in normal conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>This type of false return is relatively easy to spot on a time loop if it is due to night cooling or marine inversion as one sees very strong echoes developing over an area, spreading in size laterally but not moving and varying greatly in intensity. However, inversion of temperature exists ahead of <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">warm fronts</a> and the abnormal propagation echoes are then mixed with real rain.</p>
<p>The extreme of this problem is when the inversion is very strong and shallow, the radar beam reflects many times toward the ground as it has to follow a <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguide</a> path. This will create multiple bands of strong echoes on the radar images.</p>
<p>This situation can be found with inversions of temperature aloft or rapid decrease of moisture with height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ERAU_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ERAU-43">[43]</a></sup> In the former case, it could be difficult to notice.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Under_refraction">Under refraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Under refraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On the other hand, if the air is unstable and cools faster than the standard atmosphere with height, the beam ends up higher than expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ERAU_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-ERAU-43">[43]</a></sup> This indicates that precipitation is occurring higher than the actual height. Such an error is difficult to detect without additional temperature lapse-rate data for the area.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Rayleigh_targets">Non-Rayleigh targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Non-Rayleigh targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If we want to reliably estimate the precipitation rate, the targets have to be 10 times smaller than the radar wave according to Rayleigh scattering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-11"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> This is because the water molecule has to be excited by the radar wave to give a return. This is relatively true for rain or snow as 5 or 10 cm wavelength radars are usually employed.</p>
<p>However, for very large hydrometeors, since the wavelength is on the order of stone, the return levels off according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mie_theory" title="Mie theory">Mie theory</a>. A return of more than 55 dBZ is likely to come from hail but won't vary proportionally to the size. On the other hand, very small targets such as cloud droplets are too small to be excited and do not give a recordable return on common weather radars.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resolution_and_partially_filled_scanned_volume">Resolution and partially filled scanned volume</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Resolution and partially filled scanned volume">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-resolution.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="483" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Radar-resolution.PNG/330px-Radar-resolution.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Radar-resolution.PNG 1.5x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-resolution.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Profiler high resolution view of a thunderstorm (top) and by a weather radar (bottom).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="883" data-file-width="476" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png/170px-TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png/255px-TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png/340px-TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TDWR_and_NEXRAD_Refl_Compared_vert.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A supercell thunderstorm seen from two radars almost colocated. The top image is from a <a href="/wiki/Terminal_Doppler_Weather_Radar" title="Terminal Doppler Weather Radar">TDWR</a> and the bottom one from a <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As demonstrated at the start of the article, radar beams have a physical dimension and data are sampled at discrete angles, not continuously, along each angle of elevation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup> This results in an averaging of the values of the returns for reflectivity, velocities and polarization data on the resolution volume scanned.</p>
<p>In the figure to the left, at the top is a view of a thunderstorm taken by a <a href="/wiki/Wind_profiler" title="Wind profiler">wind profiler</a> as it was passing overhead. This is like a vertical cross section through the cloud with 150-metre vertical and 30-metre horizontal resolution. The reflectivity has large variations in a short distance. Compare this with a simulated view of what a regular weather radar would see at 60 km, in the bottom of the figure. Everything has been smoothed out. Not only the coarser resolution of the radar blur the image but the sounding incorporates area that are echo free, thus extending the thunderstorm beyond its real boundaries.</p>
<p>This shows how the output of weather radar is only an approximation of reality. The image to the right compares real data from two radars almost colocated. The <a href="/wiki/Terminal_Doppler_Weather_Radar" title="Terminal Doppler Weather Radar">TDWR</a> has about half the <a href="/wiki/Beamwidth" title="Beamwidth">beamwidth</a> of the other and one can see twice more details than with the NEXRAD.</p>
<p>Resolution can be improved by newer equipment but some things cannot. As mentioned previously, the volume scanned increases with distance so the possibility that the beam is only partially filled also increases. This leads to underestimation of the precipitation rate at larger distances and fools the user into thinking that rain is lighter as it moves away.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beam_geometry">Beam geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Beam geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The radar beam has a distribution of energy similar to the <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> pattern of a light passing through a slit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-12"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> This is because the wave is transmitted to the parabolic antenna through a slit in the wave-guide at the focal point. Most of the energy is at the center of the beam and decreases along a curve close to a Gaussian function on each side. However, there are secondary peaks of emission that will sample the targets at off-angles from the center. Designers attempt to minimize the power transmitted by such lobes, but they cannot be completely eliminated.</p>
<p>When a secondary lobe hits a reflective target such as a mountain or a strong thunderstorm, some of the energy is reflected to the radar. This energy is relatively weak but arrives at the same time that the central peak is illuminating a different azimuth. The echo is thus misplaced by the processing program. This has the effect of actually broadening the real weather echo making a smearing of weaker values on each side of it. This causes the user to overestimate the extent of the real echoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<table style="margin:auto;">
<tr>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diffraction1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="317" data-file-width="378" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Diffraction1.png/250px-Diffraction1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Diffraction1.png/375px-Diffraction1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/Diffraction1.png 2x" width="250"/></a>

</div>
</div>
</td>
<td>

</div>
</div>
