<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-weather_targets">Non-weather targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Non-weather targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is more than rain and snow in the sky. Other objects can be misinterpreted as rain or snow by weather radars. Insects and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are swept along by the prevailing winds, while birds follow their own course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> As such, fine line patterns within weather radar imagery, associated with converging winds, are dominated by insect returns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Bird migration, which tends to occur overnight within the lowest 2000 metres of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, contaminates wind profiles gathered by weather radar, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WSR-88D" title="WSR-88D">WSR-88D</a>, by increasing the environmental wind returns by 30–60 km/hr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Other objects within radar imagery include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Thin metal strips (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaff_(radar_countermeasure)" title="Chaff (radar countermeasure)">chaff</a>) dropped by military aircraft to fool enemies.</li>
<li>Solid obstacles such as mountains, buildings, and aircraft.</li>
<li>Ground and sea clutter.</li>
<li>Reflections from nearby buildings ("urban spikes").</li>
</ul>
<p>Such extraneous objects have characteristics that allow a trained eye to distinguish them. It is also possible to eliminate some of them with post-treatment of data using reflectivity, Doppler, and polarization data.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_farms">Wind farms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Wind farms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="875" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png/440px-Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png/660px-Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png 2x" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ref_Dop_wind_farm_KBUF.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reflectivity (left) and radial velocities (right) southeast of a NEXRAD weather radar. Echoes in circles are from a wind farm.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The rotating blades of <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmills</a> on modern <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a> can return the radar beam to the radar if they are in its path. Since the blades are moving, the echoes will have a velocity and can be mistaken for real precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The closer the wind farm, the stronger the return, and the combined signal from many towers is stronger. In some conditions, the radar can even see toward and away velocities that generate false positives for the <a href="/wiki/Tornado_vortex_signature" title="Tornado vortex signature">tornado vortex signature</a> algorithm on weather radar; such an event occurred in 2009 in <a href="/wiki/Dodge_City,_Kansas" title="Dodge City, Kansas">Dodge City, Kansas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>As with other structures that stand in the beam, <a href="/wiki/Attenuation" title="Attenuation">attenuation</a> of radar returns from beyond windmills may also lead to underestimation.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attenuation">Attenuation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Attenuation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:662px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attenuation.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="243" data-file-width="708" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Attenuation.PNG/660px-Attenuation.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/99/Attenuation.PNG 1.5x" width="660"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attenuation.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Example of strong attenuation when a line of <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a> moves over (from left to right images) a 5 cm wavelength weather radar (red arrow). Source: Environment Canada</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Microwaves used in weather radars can be absorbed by rain, depending on the wavelength used. For 10 cm radars, this attenuation is negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-13"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> That is the reason why countries with high water content storms are using 10 cm wavelength, for example the US NEXRAD. The cost of a larger antenna, klystron and other related equipment is offset by this benefit.</p>
<p>For a 5 cm radar, absorption becomes important in heavy rain and this attenuation leads to underestimation of echoes in and beyond a strong thunderstorm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-14"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> Canada and other northern countries use this less costly kind of radar as the precipitation in such areas is usually less intense. However, users must consider this characteristic when interpreting data. The images above show how a strong line of echoes seems to vanish as it moves over the radar. To compensate for this behaviour, radar sites are often chosen to somewhat overlap in coverage to give different points of view of the same storms.</p>
<p>Shorter wavelengths are even more attenuated and are only useful on short range<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-15"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> radar. Many television stations in the United States have 5cm radars to cover their audience area. Knowing their limitations and using them with the local NEXRAD can supplement the data available to a meteorologist.</p>
<p>Due to the spread of dual-polarization radar systems, robust and efficient approaches for the compesantion of rain attenuation are currently implemented by operational weather services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bright_band">Bright band</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Bright band">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-bright-band.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="449" data-file-width="791" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Radar-bright-band.png/330px-Radar-bright-band.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Radar-bright-band.png/495px-Radar-bright-band.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Radar-bright-band.png/660px-Radar-bright-band.png 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-bright-band.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1.5 km altitude CAPPI at the top with strong contamination from the brightband (yellows). The vertical cut at the bottom shows that this strong return is only above ground.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A radar beam's reflectivity depends on the diameter of the target and its capacity to reflect. Snowflakes are large but weakly reflective while rain drops are small but highly reflective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-16"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>When snow falls through a layer above freezing temperature, it melts into rain. Using the reflectivity equation, one can demonstrate that the returns from the snow before melting and the rain after, are not too different as the change in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">dielectric constant</a> compensates for the change in size. However, during the melting process, the radar wave "sees" something akin to very large droplets as snow flakes become coated with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-17"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>This gives enhanced returns that can be mistaken for stronger precipitations. On a PPI, this will show up as an intense ring of precipitation at the altitude where the beam crosses the melting level while on a series of CAPPIs, only the ones near that level will have stronger echoes. A good way to confirm a bright band is to make a vertical cross section through the data, as illustrated in the picture above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>An opposite problem is that drizzle (precipitation with small water droplet diameter) tends not to show up on radar because radar returns are proportional to the sixth power of droplet diameter.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_reflections">Multiple reflections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Multiple reflections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three-body-scattering.PNG"><img alt="Three-body-scattering.PNG" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="617" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Three-body-scattering.PNG/260px-Three-body-scattering.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Three-body-scattering.PNG/390px-Three-body-scattering.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Three-body-scattering.PNG/520px-Three-body-scattering.PNG 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three-body-scattering.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_body_scatter_spike" title="Three body scatter spike">Three body scatter spike</a></div>
<p>It is assumed that the beam hits the weather targets and returns directly to the radar. In fact, there is energy reflected in all directions. Most of it is weak, and multiple reflections diminish it even further so what can eventually return to the radar from such an event is negligible. However, some situations allow a multiple-reflected radar beam to be received by the radar antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doviak_12-18"><a href="#cite_note-Doviak-12">[12]</a></sup> For instance, when the beam hits hail, the energy spread toward the wet ground will be reflected back to the hail and then to the radar. The resulting echo is weak but noticeable. Due to the extra path length it has to go through, it arrives later at the antenna and is placed further than its source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemon_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lemon-52">[52]</a></sup> This gives a kind of triangle of false weaker reflections placed radially behind the hail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-EC-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Solutions_for_now_and_the_future">Solutions for now and the future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Solutions for now and the future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Filtering">Filtering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Filtering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-artefacts2.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="572" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Radar-artefacts2.PNG/220px-Radar-artefacts2.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Radar-artefacts2.PNG/330px-Radar-artefacts2.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Radar-artefacts2.PNG/440px-Radar-artefacts2.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-artefacts2.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Radar image of reflectivity with many non-weather echoes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="572" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG/220px-Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG/330px-Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG/440px-Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-artefacts_filtered.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The same image but cleaned using the Doppler velocities.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These two images show what can be presently achieved to clean up radar data. The output on the left is made with the raw returns and it is difficult to spot the real weather. Since rain and snow clouds are usually moving, one can use the Doppler velocities to eliminate a good part of the clutter (ground echoes, reflections from buildings seen as urban spikes, anomalous propagation). The image on the right has been filtered using this property.</p>
<p>However, not all non-meteorological targets remain still (birds, insects, dust). Others, like the bright band, depend on the structure of the precipitation. Polarization offers a direct typing of the echoes which could be used to filter more false data or produce separate images for specialized purposes. This recent development is expected to improve the quality of radar products.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesonet">Mesonet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Mesonet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phasedarray1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="386" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Phasedarray1.png/140px-Phasedarray1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Phasedarray1.png/210px-Phasedarray1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Phasedarray1.png/280px-Phasedarray1.png 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phasedarray1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Phased Array Weather Radar in <a href="/wiki/Norman,_Oklahoma" title="Norman, Oklahoma">Norman, Oklahoma</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another question is the resolution. As mentioned previously, radar data are an average of the scanned volume by the beam. Resolution can be improved by larger antenna or denser networks. A program by the <a href="/wiki/Engineering_Research_Center_for_Collaborative_Adaptive_Sensing_of_the_Atmosphere" title="Engineering Research Center for Collaborative Adaptive Sensing of the Atmosphere">Center for Collaborative Adaptive Sensing of the Atmosphere</a> (CASA) aims to supplement the regular <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> (a network in the United States) using many low cost X-band (3 cm) weather radar mounted on cellular telephone towers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAMS_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAMS-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> These radars will subdivide the large area of the NEXRAD into smaller domains to look at altitudes below its lowest angle. These will give details not currently available.</p>
<p>Using 3 cm radars, the antenna of each radar is small (about 1 meter diameter) but the resolution is similar at short distance to that of NEXRAD. The attenuation is significant due to the wavelength used but each point in the coverage area is seen by many radars, each viewing from a different direction and compensating for data lost from others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAMS_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-BAMS-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scanning_strategies">Scanning strategies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Scanning strategies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The number of elevation scanned and the time taken for a complete cycle depend on the weather situation. For instance, with little or no precipitation, the scheme may be limited the lowest angles and using longer impulses in order to detect wind shift near the surface. On the other hand, in violent thundertorm situations, it is better to scan on a large number of angles in order to have a 3 dimensions view of the precipitations as often as possible. To mitigate those different demands, scanning strategies have been developed according to the type of radar, the wavelength used and the most commons weather situations in the area considered.</p>
<p>One example of scanning strategies is given by the US <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD#Scan_strategies" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> radar network which has evolved with time. For instance, in 2008, it added extra resolution of data,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and in 2014, additional intra-cycle scanning of lowest level elevation (<a href="/wiki/MESO-SAILS" title="MESO-SAILS">MESO-SAILS</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic_sounding">Electronic sounding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Electronic sounding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Timeliness is also a point needing improvement. With 5 to 10 minutes time between complete scans of weather radar, much data is lost as a thunderstorm develops. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phased-array_radar" title="Phased-array radar">Phased-array radar</a> is being tested at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Lab" title="National Severe Storms Lab">National Severe Storms Lab</a> in Norman, Oklahoma, to speed the data gathering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> A team in Japan has deployed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phased-array_radar" title="Phased-array radar">phased-array radar</a> for <a href="/wiki/3D_NowCasting" title="3D NowCasting">3D NowCasting</a> at the RIKEN Advanced Institute for Computational Science (AICS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specialized_applications">Specialized applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Specialized applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg/220px-Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg/330px-Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg/440px-Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Weather_radar_on_PC-12_wing_leading_edge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Weather radar on the wing of <a href="/wiki/Pilatus_PC-12" title="Pilatus PC-12">Pilatus PC-12</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Avionics_weather_radar">Avionics weather radar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Avionics weather radar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aircraft application of radar systems include weather radar, collision avoidance, target tracking, ground proximity, and other systems. For commercial weather radar, <a href="/wiki/ARINC_708" title="ARINC 708">ARINC 708</a> is the primary specification for weather radar systems using an airborne <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">pulse-Doppler radar</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antennas">Antennas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Antennas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Unlike ground weather radar, which is set at a fixed angle, airborne weather radar is being utilized from the nose or wing of an aircraft. Not only will the aircraft be moving up, down, left, and right, but it will be rolling as well. To compensate for this, the antenna is linked and calibrated to the vertical gyro located on the aircraft. By doing this, the pilot is able to set a pitch or angle to the antenna that will enable the stabilizer to keep the antenna pointed in the right direction under moderate maneuvers. The small servo motors will not be able to keep up with abrupt maneuvers, but it will try. In doing this the pilot is able to adjust the radar so that it will point towards the weather system of interest. If the airplane is at a low altitude, the pilot would want to set the radar above the horizon line so that ground clutter is minimized on the display. If the airplane is at a very high altitude, the pilot will set the radar at a low or negative angle, to point the radar towards the clouds wherever they may be relative to the aircraft. If the airplane changes attitude, the stabilizer will adjust itself accordingly so that the pilot doesn't have to fly with one hand and adjust the radar with the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bendix_Corporation_1973_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix_Corporation_1973-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Receivers/transmitters"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Receivers.2Ftransmitters">Receivers/transmitters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Receivers/transmitters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are two major systems when talking about the receiver/transmitter: the first is high-powered systems, and the second is low-powered systems; both of which operate in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-band" title="X-band">X-band</a> frequency range (8,000 – 12,500 MHz). High-powered systems operate at 10,000 – 60,000 watts. These systems consist of magnetrons that are fairly expensive (approximately $1,700) and allow for considerable noise due to irregularities with the system. Thus, these systems are highly dangerous for arcing and are not safe to be used around ground personnel. However, the alternative would be the low-powered systems. These systems operate 100 – 200 watts, and require a combination of high gain receivers, signal microprocessors, and transistors to operate as effectively as the high-powered systems. The complex microprocessors help to eliminate noise, providing a more accurate and detailed depiction of the sky. Also, since there are fewer irregularities throughout the system, the low-powered radars can be used to detect turbulence via the Doppler Effect. Since low-powered systems operate at considerable less wattage, they are safe from arcing and can be used at virtually all times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bendix_Corporation_1973_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bendix_Corporation_1973-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thunderstorm_tracking">Thunderstorm tracking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Thunderstorm tracking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nowcasting_(meteorology)" title="Nowcasting (meteorology)">Nowcasting</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nowcast_ConvectiveWXfcst_ncwf2-310W.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="310" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Nowcast_ConvectiveWXfcst_ncwf2-310W.jpg/220px-Nowcast_ConvectiveWXfcst_ncwf2-310W.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Nowcast_ConvectiveWXfcst_ncwf2-310W.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nowcast_ConvectiveWXfcst_ncwf2-310W.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Weather_forecasting#Nowcasting" title="Weather forecasting">Nowcasting</a> a line of thunderstorms from <i>AutoNowcaster</i> system</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Digital radar systems now have capabilities far beyond that of their predecessors. Digital systems now offer <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a> tracking surveillance. This provides users with the ability to acquire detailed information of each storm cloud being tracked. Thunderstorms are first identified by matching precipitation raw data received from the radar pulse to some sort of template preprogrammed into the system. In order for a thunderstorm to be identified, it has to meet strict definitions of intensity and shape that set it apart from any non-convective cloud. Usually, it must show signs of organization in the horizontal and continuity in the vertical: a core or a more intense center to be identified and tracked by digital <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Radar_tracking_system&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Radar tracking system (page does not exist)">radar trackers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWC_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-TWC-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Once the thunderstorm cell is identified, speed, distance covered, direction, and Estimated Time of Arrival (ETA) are all tracked and recorded to be utilized later.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doppler_radar_and_bird_migration">Doppler radar and bird migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Doppler radar and bird migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Using the Doppler weather radar is not limited to determine the location and velocity of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>, but it can track <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">bird migrations</a> as well as seen in the <a href="#Non-weather_targets">non-weather targets</a> section. The <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> sent out by the radars bounce off rain and birds alike (or even insects like <a href="/wiki/Butterfly" title="Butterfly">butterflies</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The US <i><a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a></i>, for instance, have reported having the flights of birds appear on their radars as clouds and then fade away when the birds land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The U.S. National Weather Service St. Louis has even reported <a href="/wiki/Monarch_butterfly" title="Monarch butterfly">monarch butterflies</a> appearing on their radars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Different programs in North America use regular weather radars and specialized radar data to determine the paths, height of flight, and timing of migrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This is useful information in planning for windmill farms placement and operation, to reduce bird fatalities, aviation safety and other wildlife management. In Europe, there has been similar developments and even a comprehensive forecast program for aviation safety, based on radar detection. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOLBO161_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-GOLBO161-9">[9]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorite_fall_detection">Meteorite fall detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Weather_radar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Meteorite fall detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="966" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png/220px-KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png/330px-KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png/440px-KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KLOT_0547_14_6_deg_vel.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NOAA NEXRAD radar image of the Park Forest, IL, meteorite fall of 26 March 2003.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/American_Meteor_Society" title="American Meteor Society">American Meteor Society</a>, meteorite falls occur on a daily basis somewhere on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> However, the database of worldwide <a href="/wiki/Meteorite_fall" title="Meteorite fall">meteorite falls</a> maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Meteoritical_Society" title="Meteoritical Society">Meteoritical Society</a> typically records only about 10-15 new meteorite falls annually<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meteorite occur when a <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroid</a> falls into the Earth's atmosphere, generating an optically bright meteor by ionization and frictional heating. If the meteoroid is large enough and infall velocity is low enough, surviving meteorites will reach the ground. When the falling meteorites decelerate below about 2–4 km/s, usually at an altitude between 15 and 25 km, they no longer generate an optically bright meteor and enter "dark flight". Because of this, most meteorite falls occurring into the oceans, during the day, or otherwise go unnoticed.</p>
<p>It is in dark flight that falling meteorites typically fall through the interaction volumes of most types of radars. It as been demonstrated that it is possible to identify falling meteorites in weather radar imagery by different studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> This is especially useful for meteorite recovery, as weather radar are part of widespread networks and scan the atmosphere continuously. Furthermore, the meteorites are cause a perturbance of local winds by turbulence, which is noticeable on Doppler outputs, and are falling nearly vertically so their resting place on the ground is close to their radar signature.</p>
<p>An example is shown in the image on the right, showing the <a href="/wiki/Park_Forest_(meteorite)" title="Park Forest (meteorite)">Park Forest, IL meteorite fall</a> which occurred on 26 March 2003. The red/green feature at upper left is the motion of clouds near the radar itself, and a signature of falling meteorites is seen inside the yellow ellipse at image center. The intermixed red and green pixels indicate turbulence, in this case arising from the wakes of falling, high-velocity meteorites.</p>
